<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Musical artist</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header">Signature</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:MF_Doom_signature.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="MF Doom" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/MF_Doom_signature.svg/150px-MF_Doom_signature.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="94" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/MF_Doom_signature.svg/225px-MF_Doom_signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/MF_Doom_signature.svg/300px-MF_Doom_signature.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="149" data-file-height="93" /></a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Daniel Dumile</b><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">DOO</span>-mə-lay</i></a>; July 13, 1971<sup id="cite_ref-okp_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-okp-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>&#160;&#8211;&#32;October 31, 2020), best known by his stage name <b>MF Doom</b> or simply <b>Doom</b> (both stylized in <a href="/wiki/All_caps" title="All caps">all caps</a>), was a British-American  rapper and record producer. Noted for his intricate wordplay, signature metal mask, and "<a href="/wiki/Supervillain" title="Supervillain">supervillain</a>" stage persona, Dumile became a major figure of <a href="/wiki/Underground_hip_hop" title="Underground hip hop">underground hip hop</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alternative_hip_hop" title="Alternative hip hop">alternative hip hop</a> in the 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-weingarten2021_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weingarten2021-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lester2012_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lester2012-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> After his death, <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i> described him as one of the scene's "most celebrated, unpredictable and enigmatic figures".<sup id="cite_ref-varietyobit_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-varietyobit-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile was born in London and moved to <a href="/wiki/Long_Island,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Long Island, New York">Long Island, New York</a>, at a young age. He began his career in 1988 as a member of <a href="/wiki/KMD" title="KMD">KMD</a>, performing as <b>Zev&#160;Love&#160;X</b>. The group disbanded in 1993 after the death of member <a href="/wiki/DJ_Subroc" title="DJ Subroc">DJ Subroc</a>, Dumile's brother. After a hiatus, Dumile reemerged in the late 1990s. He began performing at <a href="/wiki/Open_mic" title="Open mic">open mic</a> events while wearing a metal mask resembling that of the <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a> supervillain <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Doom" title="Doctor Doom">Doctor Doom</a>, who is depicted on the cover of his 1999 debut solo album <i><a href="/wiki/Operation:_Doomsday" title="Operation: Doomsday">Operation: Doomsday</a></i>. He adopted the MF Doom persona and rarely made unmasked public appearances thereafter.
</p><p>Between 2003 and 2005, Dumile released four solo albums and three collaborative albums. In addition to the critically acclaimed <i><a href="/wiki/Mm..Food" title="Mm..Food">Mm..Food</a></i> (2004) under the MF Doom moniker, he released solo albums including one under the pseudonym <b>King Geedorah</b> and two as <b>Viktor Vaughn</b>. <i><a href="/wiki/Madvillainy" title="Madvillainy">Madvillainy</a></i> (2004), recorded with the producer <a href="/wiki/Madlib" title="Madlib">Madlib</a> under the name <a href="/wiki/Madvillain" title="Madvillain">Madvillain</a>, is often cited as Dumile's <i><a href="/wiki/Magnum_opus" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnum opus">magnum opus</a></i> and is regarded as a landmark album in hip hop.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, Dumile released <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mouse_and_the_Mask" title="The Mouse and the Mask">The Mouse and the Mask</a></i> with the producer <a href="/wiki/Danger_Mouse_(music_producer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Danger Mouse (music producer)">Danger Mouse</a> as <a href="/wiki/Danger_Doom" title="Danger Doom">Danger Doom</a>.
</p><p>Though he lived the majority of his life in the United States, Dumile never gained American citizenship. In 2010, he was denied reentry after returning from an international tour for his sixth and final solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/Born_Like_This" title="Born Like This">Born Like This</a></i> (2009). He relocated to London and, in his final years, worked mostly in collaboration with other artists, releasing albums with <a href="/wiki/Jneiro_Jarel" title="Jneiro Jarel">Jneiro Jarel</a> (as <a href="/wiki/JJ_Doom" title="JJ Doom">JJ Doom</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bishop_Nehru" title="Bishop Nehru">Bishop Nehru</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/NehruvianDoom" title="NehruvianDoom">NehruvianDoom</a></i>), and <a href="/wiki/Czarface" title="Czarface">Czarface</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Czarface_Meets_Metal_Face" title="Czarface Meets Metal Face">Czarface Meets Metal Face</a></i> and the posthumous <i><a href="/wiki/Super_What%3F" title="Super What?">Super What?</a></i>). Dumile died in 2020 from <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">angioedema</a> following a reaction to a <a href="/wiki/ACE_inhibitor" title="ACE inhibitor">blood pressure medication</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Dumile was born in <a href="/wiki/Hounslow" title="Hounslow">Hounslow</a>, London, on July 13, 1971,<sup id="cite_ref-yoo2021_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoo2021-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc20201231_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20201231-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-okp_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-okp-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> the son of a <a href="/wiki/Trinidadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinidadian">Trinidadian</a> mother and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean" class="mw-redirect" title="Zimbabwean">Zimbabwean</a> father.<sup id="cite_ref-lester2012_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lester2012-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He was the eldest of five children.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> According to Dumile, he was conceived in the United States, and happened to be born in London because his mother was visiting family.<sup id="cite_ref-Mlynar_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mlynar-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> As a child, Dumile moved with his family to <a href="/wiki/Long_Island,_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Long Island, New York">Long Island, New York</a>, and grew up in <a href="/wiki/Long_Beach,_New_York" title="Long Beach, New York">Long Beach</a> on Long Island.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> He said he had no memory of his London childhood and his parents had no affiliation with British culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Mlynar_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mlynar-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> However, he remained a British citizen, never gaining American citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile began DJing during the summer after third grade.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> As a child, he was a fan and collector of comic books and earned the nickname "Doom" (a phonetic play on the name Dumile) among friends and family.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<h3><span id="1988.E2.80.931997:_KMD.2C_Subroc.27s_death.2C_and_hiatus"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1988–1997:_KMD,_Subroc's_death,_and_hiatus">1988–1997: KMD, Subroc's death, and hiatus</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:KMD_Elektra.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6e/KMD_Elektra.jpg/220px-KMD_Elektra.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/6e/KMD_Elektra.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="283" data-file-height="354" /></a><figcaption>Dumile as Zev Love X (left) with fellow <a href="/wiki/KMD" title="KMD">KMD</a> members <a href="/wiki/DJ_Subroc" title="DJ Subroc">DJ Subroc</a> and Onyx the Birthstone Kid in 1991</figcaption></figure>
<p>As Zev Love X,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Dumile formed the hip hop group <a href="/wiki/KMD" title="KMD">KMD</a> in 1988 with his younger brother <a href="/wiki/DJ_Subroc" title="DJ Subroc">DJ Subroc</a> and Rodan, who was later replaced by Onyx the Birthstone Kid.<sup id="cite_ref-ALLMUSIC_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ALLMUSIC-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Artists_and_repertoire" title="Artists and repertoire">A&amp;R</a> representative Dante Ross learned of KMD through the hip hop group <a href="/wiki/3rd_Bass" title="3rd Bass">3rd Bass</a> and signed them to <a href="/wiki/Elektra_Records" title="Elektra Records">Elektra Records</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wax_poetics_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wax_poetics-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Their recording debut came on 3rd Bass's song "The Gas Face" on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cactus_Album" title="The Cactus Album">The Cactus Album</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-ALLMUSIC_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ALLMUSIC-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> followed in 1991 by their debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Hood" title="Mr. Hood">Mr. Hood</a></i>. Dumile performed the last verse on "The Gas Face"; according to <a href="/wiki/Pete_Nice" title="Pete Nice">Pete Nice</a>'s verse on the track, Dumile created the phrase.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 23, 1993, just before the release of the second KMD album, <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Bastards" title="Black Bastards">Black Bastards</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-ALLMUSIC_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ALLMUSIC-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Subroc was struck by a car and killed while crossing the <a href="/wiki/Long_Island_Expressway" class="mw-redirect" title="Long Island Expressway">Long Island Expressway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Dumile completed the album alone over the course of several months, and it was announced with a release date of May 3, 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> KMD was dropped by Elektra and the album went unreleased due to its controversial cover art,<sup id="cite_ref-wax_poetics_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wax_poetics-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> which featured a cartoon of a stereotypical <a href="/wiki/Pickaninny" title="Pickaninny">pickaninny</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sambo_(racial_term)" title="Sambo (racial term)">sambo</a> character being hanged.<sup id="cite_ref-ducker2014_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ducker2014-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After his brother's death, Dumile retreated from the hip hop scene from 1994 to 1997, living "damn near homeless, walking the streets of <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, sleeping on benches".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu200548_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu200548-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In the late 1990s, he settled in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>; he had moved to Georgia in the mid-90s.<sup id="cite_ref-yoo2021_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoo2021-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> According to interviews with Dumile, he was "recovering from his wounds" and swearing revenge "against the industry that so badly deformed him".<sup id="cite_ref-ALLMUSIC_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ALLMUSIC-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <i>Black Bastards</i> had been <a href="/wiki/Bootleg_recording" title="Bootleg recording">bootlegged</a> by that time,<sup id="cite_ref-ducker2014_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ducker2014-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> but was not officially released until 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBradleyDuBois2010606_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBradleyDuBois2010606-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1997.E2.80.932001:_Operation:_Doomsday_and_production_work"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1997–2001:_Operation:_Doomsday_and_production_work">1997–2001: <i>Operation: Doomsday</i> and production work</span></h3>
<p>In 1997 or 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> Dumile began freestyling incognito at <a href="/wiki/Open_mic" title="Open mic">open-mic</a> events at the <a href="/wiki/Nuyorican_Poets_Caf%C3%A9" title="Nuyorican Poets Café">Nuyorican Poets Café</a> in Manhattan, obscuring his face by putting tights over his head.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc20201231_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20201231-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> He turned this into a new identity, MF Doom, with a mask similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a> supervillain <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Doom" title="Doctor Doom">Doctor Doom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung201459_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung201459-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> He later used a mask based on the character Maximus from the 2000 film <i><a href="/wiki/Gladiator_(2000_film)" title="Gladiator (2000 film)">Gladiator</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bobbito_Garcia" title="Bobbito Garcia">Bobbito Garcia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fondle_%27Em_Records" title="Fondle &#39;Em Records">Fondle 'Em Records</a> released <i><a href="/wiki/Operation:_Doomsday" title="Operation: Doomsday">Operation: Doomsday</a></i>, Dumile's first full-length <a href="/wiki/LP_record" title="LP record">LP</a> as MF Doom, in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-allah2020_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allah2020-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Dumile's collaborators on <i>Operation: Doomsday</i> included fellow members of the <a href="/wiki/Monsta_Island_Czars" title="Monsta Island Czars">Monsta Island Czars</a> collective, for which each artist took on the persona of a monster from the <a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(franchise)" title="Godzilla (franchise)"><i>Godzilla</i></a> films. Dumile went by the alias "King Geedorah",<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> a three-headed golden dragon space monster modeled after <a href="/wiki/King_Ghidorah" title="King Ghidorah">King Ghidorah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The album's productions sampled cartoons including <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four_(1967_TV_series)" title="Fantastic Four (1967 TV series)"><i>Fantastic Four</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-allah2020_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allah2020-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jon_Caramanica" title="Jon Caramanica">Jon Caramanica</a>, in a review of <i>Operation: Doomsday</i> for <i><a href="/wiki/Spin_(magazine)" title="Spin (magazine)">Spin</a></i>, emphasized the contrast between Dumile's <a href="/wiki/Rapping#Flow" title="Rapping">flow</a> as Zev Love X in KMD and his revised approach as a solo artist: "Doom's flow is muddy, nowhere near the sprightly rhymes of KMD's early days, and his thought process is haphazard."<sup id="cite_ref-:7_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Caramanica revisited <i>Operation: Doomsday</i> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> in 2021, calling it "one of the most idiosyncratic hip-hop albums of the 1990s, and one of the defining documents of the independent hip-hop explosion of that decade".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Cyril Cordor, in a review for <i><a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a></i>, described <i>Operation: Doomsday</i> as Dumile's "rawest" lyrical effort.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, Dumile began releasing his <i><a href="/wiki/Special_Herbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Special Herbs">Special Herbs</a></i> instrumentals series under the pseudonym Metal Fingers.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In a review of a 2011 box set containing ten volumes of the <i>Special Herbs</i> series, <i><a href="/wiki/Pitchfork_(website)" title="Pitchfork (website)">Pitchfork</a></i> observed that the instrumentals stand on their own without vocal tracks: "most of these tracks sound plenty 'finished' even in rhyme-less form".<sup id="cite_ref-:5_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2002.E2.80.932004:_King_Geedorah.2C_Viktor_Vaughn.2C_and_Madvillainy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2002–2004:_King_Geedorah,_Viktor_Vaughn,_and_Madvillainy">2002–2004: King Geedorah, Viktor Vaughn, and <i>Madvillainy</i></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Madlib_in_2003.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Photo of a man with a short goatee and mustache wearing a durag" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Madlib_in_2003.jpg/220px-Madlib_in_2003.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Madlib_in_2003.jpg/330px-Madlib_in_2003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Madlib_in_2003.jpg/440px-Madlib_in_2003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="806" data-file-height="645" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Madlib" title="Madlib">Madlib</a> in late 2003, around the time he was working on <i><a href="/wiki/Madvillainy" title="Madvillainy">Madvillainy</a></i> with Dumile</figcaption></figure><p>In 2003, Dumile released the album <i><a href="/wiki/Take_Me_to_Your_Leader_(King_Geedorah_album)" title="Take Me to Your Leader (King Geedorah album)">Take Me to Your Leader</a></i> under his King Geedorah moniker.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In <i>Pitchfork</i>, Mark Martelli described <i>Take Me to Your Leader</i> as close to a <a href="/wiki/Concept_album" title="Concept album">concept album</a>, noting how it lays out the "mythos" of the eponymous King Geedorah.<sup id="cite_ref-martellitmtyl_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martellitmtyl-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Martelli praised the album, particularly tracks such as "One Smart Nigger" which, in his view, were superior to other artists' attempts at <a href="/wiki/Political_hip_hop" title="Political hip hop">political hip hop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-martellitmtyl_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martellitmtyl-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Fact_(UK_magazine)" title="Fact (UK magazine)">Fact</a></i>, in a brief notice for a 2013 <a href="/wiki/Reissue" title="Reissue">reissue</a> of <i>Take Me to Your Leader</i>, called it "arguably the most cinematic" of Dumile's albums from the turn of the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later in 2003, Dumile released the LP <i><a href="/wiki/Vaudeville_Villain" title="Vaudeville Villain">Vaudeville Villain</a></i> under the moniker Viktor Vaughn (another play on Doctor Doom, who is also known as Victor von Doom). <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> described the Viktor Vaughn persona as "a time travelling street hustler".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <i>Pitchfork</i> named <i>Vaudeville Villain</i> the week's best new album and highlighted its lyricism, writing that Dumile was one of the best writers in rap.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile's breakthrough came in 2004 with the album <i><a href="/wiki/Madvillainy" title="Madvillainy">Madvillainy</a></i>, created with producer <a href="/wiki/Madlib" title="Madlib">Madlib</a> under the group name <a href="/wiki/Madvillain" title="Madvillain">Madvillain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> They recorded the album in a series of sessions over two years before a commercial release on March 23, 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <i>Madvillainy</i> was a critical and commercial success,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and has since become known as Dumile's masterpiece.<sup id="cite_ref-guardianobit_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianobit-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 2004, Dumile released <i><a href="/wiki/VV:2" title="VV:2">VV:2</a></i>, a follow-up LP under the Viktor Vaughn moniker. <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Rabin" title="Nathan Rabin">Nathan Rabin</a> noted in <i><a href="/wiki/The_A.V._Club" title="The A.V. Club">The A.V. Club</a></i> that <i>VV:2</i>, coming as it did after the commercial and critical success of <i>Madvillainy</i>, represented an unusual career choice for Dumile whereby he went "deeper underground" instead of embracing wider fame.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later in 2004, the second MF Doom album <i><a href="/wiki/Mm..Food" title="Mm..Food">Mm..Food</a></i> was released by <a href="/wiki/Rhymesayers_Entertainment" title="Rhymesayers Entertainment">Rhymesayers Entertainment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guardianobit_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianobit-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <i>Pitchfork</i> gave the album a positive review.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Nathan Rabin described it as a "crazy pastiche" but argued that it grew more coherent on repeated listening.
</p>
<h3><span id="2005.E2.80.932009:_Danger_Doom.2C_Born_Like_This.2C_and_Ghostface_collaboration"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2005–2009:_Danger_Doom,_Born_Like_This,_and_Ghostface_collaboration">2005–2009: Danger Doom, <i>Born Like This</i>, and Ghostface collaboration</span></h3>
<p>Although still an independent artist, Dumile took a bigger step towards the mainstream in 2005 with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mouse_and_the_Mask" title="The Mouse and the Mask">The Mouse and the Mask</a></i>, a collaboration with the producer <a href="/wiki/DJ_Danger_Mouse" class="mw-redirect" title="DJ Danger Mouse">DJ Danger Mouse</a> under the group name <a href="/wiki/Danger_Doom" title="Danger Doom">Danger Doom</a>. The album, released on October 11, 2005, by <a href="/wiki/Epitaph_Records" title="Epitaph Records">Epitaph</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lex_Records" title="Lex Records">Lex</a>, was developed in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Network" title="Cartoon Network">Cartoon Network</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Adult_Swim" title="Adult Swim">Adult Swim</a> and featured voice actors and characters from its programs (mostly <i><a href="/wiki/Aqua_Teen_Hunger_Force" title="Aqua Teen Hunger Force">Aqua Teen Hunger Force</a></i>). <i>The Mouse and the Mask</i> reached #41 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Critic Chris Vognar, discussing the role of comedy in hip hop, argued that "Doom and Danger exemplify an absurdist strain in recent independent hip-hop, a willingness to embrace the nerdy without a heavy cloak of irony".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVognar2011120_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVognar2011120-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the same year, Dumile appeared on the second <a href="/wiki/Gorillaz" title="Gorillaz">Gorillaz</a> album, <i><a href="/wiki/Demon_Days" title="Demon Days">Demon Days</a>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-bbc20201231_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20201231-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile produced tracks for both of <a href="/wiki/Ghostface_Killah" title="Ghostface Killah">Ghostface Killah</a>'s 2006 albums <i><a href="/wiki/Fishscale" title="Fishscale">Fishscale</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/More_Fish" title="More Fish">More Fish</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In February 2013, Ghostface Killah said that he and Dumile were in the process of choosing tracks for a collaborative album.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, Ghostface Killah announced that the album, <i>Swift &amp; Changeable</i>, would be released in 2016, and later posted promotional artwork for the collaboration.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> It remains unreleased.
</p><p>Dumile's <i><a href="/wiki/Born_Like_This" title="Born Like This">Born Like This</a></i> was released on Lex Records on March 24, 2009. The album was Dumile's first solo album to chart in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-lex_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lex-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In a largely favorable review for <i>Pitchfork</i>, Nate Patrin cast the album as a return to form for Dumile, following a period of limited output.<sup id="cite_ref-patrinbornlikethis_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patrinbornlikethis-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> He observed that Dumile's lyrics and flow—"a focused rasp that's subtly grown slightly more ragged and intense"—were darker than on earlier records.<sup id="cite_ref-patrinbornlikethis_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patrinbornlikethis-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> He also highlighted the overtly homophobic "Batty Boyz", a <a href="/wiki/Diss_(music)" title="Diss (music)">diss track</a> against unnamed rappers.<sup id="cite_ref-patrinbornlikethis_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patrinbornlikethis-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Steve Yates, reviewing the album in <i>The Guardian</i>, likewise saw <i>Born Like This</i> as hearkening back to Dumile's earlier output.<sup id="cite_ref-yatesbornlikethis_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yatesbornlikethis-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>  Yates felt it presented Dumile at "his scalpel-tongued, scatter-mouthed best".<sup id="cite_ref-yatesbornlikethis_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yatesbornlikethis-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Both Patrin and Yates noted the influence of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bukowski" title="Charles Bukowski">Charles Bukowski</a> on <i>Born Like This</i>: the first line of Bukowski's poem "Dinosauria, We" gives the album its title.<sup id="cite_ref-patrinbornlikethis_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patrinbornlikethis-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yatesbornlikethis_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yatesbornlikethis-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2010.E2.80.932021:_Move_to_London_and_later_collaborations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2010–2021:_Move_to_London_and_later_collaborations">2010–2021: Move to London and later collaborations</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:MF_Doom_The_Arches_3.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Photo portrait of a man wearing a golden mask and hoodie, holding a sampler and pointing at the viewer" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MF_Doom_The_Arches_3.jpg/220px-MF_Doom_The_Arches_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MF_Doom_The_Arches_3.jpg/330px-MF_Doom_The_Arches_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/MF_Doom_The_Arches_3.jpg/440px-MF_Doom_The_Arches_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3328" data-file-height="4992" /></a><figcaption>Dumile in 2008</figcaption></figure>
<p>In early 2010, Dumile released the EP <i><a href="/wiki/Gazzillion_Ear" title="Gazzillion Ear">Gazzillion Ear</a></i> on Lex, a compilation of remixes of "Gazzillion Ear" from <i>Born Like This,</i> including a remix by <a href="/wiki/Thom_Yorke" title="Thom Yorke">Thom Yorke</a> and two mixes by <a href="/wiki/Jneiro_Jarel" title="Jneiro Jarel">Jneiro Jarel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> A further remix by Madvillain featuring a voicemail message from <a href="/wiki/Kanye_West" title="Kanye West">Kanye West</a> was released online.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The EP coincided with Dumile's first performances outside North America. On March 5, 2010, Lex and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B3nar" title="Sónar">Sónar</a> presented the first Doom show in London, at the <a href="/wiki/Roundhouse_(venue)" title="Roundhouse (venue)">Roundhouse</a> in Camden.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Expektoration" title="Expektoration">Expektoration</a></i>, Dumile's second live album, was released on September 14, 2010, through Gold Dust.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In a review of <i>Expektoration</i>, <i>Pitchfork</i> noted that Dumile's vocal performance was more energetic than on his recordings, which it characterized as "laidback" by comparison.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After completing his European tour, Dumile was refused re-entry into the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-faceddeportation_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faceddeportation-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> He settled in the UK in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Key_to_the_Kuffs" title="Key to the Kuffs">Key to the Kuffs</a></i>, an album Dumile made in collaboration with the producer <a href="/wiki/Jneiro_Jarel" title="Jneiro Jarel">Jneiro Jarel</a> as <a href="/wiki/JJ_Doom" title="JJ Doom">JJ Doom</a>, was released on August 20, 2012, and included guest features from <a href="/wiki/Damon_Albarn" title="Damon Albarn">Damon Albarn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beth_Gibbons" title="Beth Gibbons">Beth Gibbons</a> of Portishead, <a href="/wiki/Khujo" title="Khujo">Khujo</a> Goodie of <a href="/wiki/Goodie_Mob" title="Goodie Mob">Goodie Mob</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dungeon_Family" title="Dungeon Family">Dungeon Family</a>, and Boston Fielder.<sup id="cite_ref-complex_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-complex-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Reviews of <i>Key to the Kuffs</i> in <i>Pitchfork</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fact_(UK_magazine)" title="Fact (UK magazine)">Fact</a></i> emphasized its references to Dumile's "exile" in the United Kingdom,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/Resident_Advisor" title="Resident Advisor">Resident Advisor</a></i> noted its play on Britishisms in tracks like "Guv'nor".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/NehruvianDoom" title="NehruvianDoom">NehruvianDoom</a></i>, Dumile's collaboration with the rapper <a href="/wiki/Bishop_Nehru" title="Bishop Nehru">Bishop Nehru</a>, was released on October 7, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Dumile produced all the tracks on <i>NehruvianDoom</i>, often using beats developed in the <i>Special Herbs</i> series; vocals are primarily Nehru's, with some contributions from Dumile.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The album was Nehru's major label debut.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The limitations of Nehru's artistic achievement on the album were stressed by critics due to his relative youth (he was still in his teens when the album was produced) and the album's briefness, lasting just over 30 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Dumile's contributions were also seen as limited: <i>Pitchfork</i> wrote that he often seemed on "autopilot",<sup id="cite_ref-:2_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/XXL_(magazine)" title="XXL (magazine)"><i>XXL</i></a> suggested that neither he nor Nehru were able to "push the envelope".<sup id="cite_ref-:4_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2017, Adult Swim announced a Doom compilation, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Missing_Notebook_Rhymes" title="The Missing Notebook Rhymes">The Missing Notebook Rhymes</a></i>, that would consist of songs from his upcoming projects and featured appearances on other artists' songs. The Adult Swim website was to release one new song per week over the course of 15 weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> However, the arrangement was canceled in September after the release of only seven tracks.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2018, Dumile and <a href="/wiki/Czarface" title="Czarface">Czarface</a> released "Nautical Depth", the first single from their collaborative album <i><a href="/wiki/Czarface_Meets_Metal_Face" title="Czarface Meets Metal Face">Czarface Meets Metal Face</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The album was released on March 30, 2018. In a lukewarm review for <i>Pitchfork</i>, Mehan Jayasuriya compared verses by <a href="/wiki/Open_Mike_Eagle" title="Open Mike Eagle">Open Mike Eagle</a> favorably to Dumile's, but noted that Dumile's contribution to "Nautical Depth" exhibited his "once razor-sharp lyricism".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Ben Beaumont-Thomas, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, was more positive, noting Dumile's "stoner surrealism" in "Captain Crunch".<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from the album with Czarface, Dumile's musical output in the final three years of his life was limited to one-off guest appearances on other artists' tracks.<sup id="cite_ref-Sayles_2021_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sayles_2021-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Posthumous releases included appearances on two songs for the video game <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Theft_Auto_Online" title="Grand Theft Auto Online">Grand Theft Auto Online</a></i>: "Lunch Break", with <a href="/wiki/Flying_Lotus" title="Flying Lotus">Flying Lotus</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and "The Chocolate Conquistadors", with <a href="/wiki/BadBadNotGood" title="BadBadNotGood">BadBadNotGood</a>, made for the game's content update <i>The Cayo Perico Heist.</i><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after Dumile's death was announced, Flying Lotus revealed that they had been working on an EP.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Having been completed in early 2020 but later delayed due to the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>, Dumile's second collaborative album with Czarface and first posthumous release, <i><a href="/wiki/Super_What%3F" title="Super What?">Super What?</a></i>, was released in May 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_and_artistry">Style and artistry</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:MF_Doom_(2011_portrait_illustration_from_tour_poster).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Portrait illustration of a man with thinning hair wearing a metal mask and T-shirt" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MF_Doom_%282011_portrait_illustration_from_tour_poster%29.jpg/220px-MF_Doom_%282011_portrait_illustration_from_tour_poster%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MF_Doom_%282011_portrait_illustration_from_tour_poster%29.jpg/330px-MF_Doom_%282011_portrait_illustration_from_tour_poster%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MF_Doom_%282011_portrait_illustration_from_tour_poster%29.jpg/440px-MF_Doom_%282011_portrait_illustration_from_tour_poster%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1317" data-file-height="1974" /></a><figcaption>Portrait illustration of Dumile from a poster promoting his 2011 <i>Born Like This</i> tour of the UK</figcaption></figure><p>Dumile's lyrics are known for <a href="/wiki/Word_play" title="Word play">wordplay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytobit_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytobit-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Bradley and DuBois, describing Dumile as "among the most enigmatic figures in hip-hop", wrote that Dumile's "raspy baritone weaves an intricate web of allusions drawn from comic books and metaphysics along with seeming nonsense and non sequiturs".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBradleyDuBois2010606_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBradleyDuBois2010606-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> According to an obituary in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ringer_(website)" title="The Ringer (website)">The Ringer</a></i>, his <a href="/wiki/Rapping#Flow" title="Rapping">flow</a> was "loose and conversational, but delivered with technical precision", and his use of rhyme and meter eclipsed that of <a href="/wiki/Big_Pun" title="Big Pun">Big Pun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eminem" title="Eminem">Eminem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sayles_2021_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sayles_2021-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile's production work frequently incorporated samples and quotations from film.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytobit_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytobit-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> A review of <i>Special Herbs</i> volumes 5 and 6 in <a href="/wiki/CMJ#CMJ_New_Music_Monthly" title="CMJ"><i>CMJ New Music Monthly</i></a> compared Dumile's beats to "<a href="/wiki/Soul_jazz" title="Soul jazz">soul jazz</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="MF_Doom_persona">MF Doom persona</span></h3>
<p>Dumile created the MF Doom character as an <a href="/wiki/Alter_ego" title="Alter ego">alter ego</a> with a backstory he could reference in his music.<sup id="cite_ref-NEWYORKER2_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEWYORKER2-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The character combines elements from the <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a> supervillain <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Doom" title="Doctor Doom">Doctor Doom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Destro" title="Destro">Destro</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Erik_(The_Phantom_of_the_Opera)" title="Erik (The Phantom of the Opera)">Phantom of the Opera</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Lyons2019_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons2019-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> like Doctor Doom and Phantom, Dumile <a href="/wiki/Illeism" title="Illeism">referred to himself in the third person</a> while in character.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> His signature mask was similar to that of Doctor Doom,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung201459_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung201459-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> who is depicted rapping on the cover of Dumile's 1999 debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Operation:_Doomsday" title="Operation: Doomsday">Operation: Doomsday</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile wore the mask while performing, and would not be photographed without it, except for short glimpses in videos and in earlier photos with KMD.<sup id="cite_ref-ALLMUSIC2_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ALLMUSIC2-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Later versions of the mask were based on a prop from the 2000 film <i><a href="/wiki/Gladiator_(2000_film)" title="Gladiator (2000 film)">Gladiator</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Academic Hershini Bhana Young argued that, by appropriating the Doctor Doom mask, Dumile "positions himself as enemy, not only of the music industry but also of dominant constructions of identity that relegate him as a black man to second-class citizenship".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung201459_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung201459-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile sometimes sent stand-ins to perform in the mask, which he saw as a "logical extension" of the concept but angered audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-NEWYORKER2_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEWYORKER2-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Dumile initially claimed that he had lost weight and thus looked and sounded different.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> At a 2010 show in Toronto, an imposter was booed off stage before being replaced by Dumile.<sup id="cite_ref-intentionalimpostor2_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-intentionalimpostor2-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, Dumile described himself as the "writer and director" of the character and that he "might send a white dude next ... Whoever plays the character plays the character."<sup id="cite_ref-NEWYORKER2_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEWYORKER2-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2019, during his performance at the <a href="/wiki/Adult_Swim" title="Adult Swim">Adult Swim</a> Festival, the electronic artist <a href="/wiki/Flying_Lotus" title="Flying Lotus">Flying Lotus</a> announced that he would be joined onstage by Dumile. Instead, the masked figure who appeared on stage was revealed as the comedian <a href="/wiki/Hannibal_Buress" title="Hannibal Buress">Hannibal Buress</a>. Dumile's involvement in the prank has not been confirmed.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_influence">Legacy and influence</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:MF_DOOM_Tribute_Deptford_Creekside.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/MF_DOOM_Tribute_Deptford_Creekside.jpg/220px-MF_DOOM_Tribute_Deptford_Creekside.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/MF_DOOM_Tribute_Deptford_Creekside.jpg/330px-MF_DOOM_Tribute_Deptford_Creekside.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/MF_DOOM_Tribute_Deptford_Creekside.jpg/440px-MF_DOOM_Tribute_Deptford_Creekside.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3130" data-file-height="2075" /></a><figcaption>Mural depicting MF Doom in <a href="/wiki/Deptford" title="Deptford">Deptford</a>, London</figcaption></figure>
<p>Dumile is considered one of the most celebrated and influential musicians in hip hop.<sup id="cite_ref-varietyobit_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-varietyobit-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The English musician <a href="/wiki/Thom_Yorke" title="Thom Yorke">Thom Yorke</a>, who collaborated with Dumile on two occasions, wrote: "He was a massive inspiration to so many of us, changed things... For me the way he put words was often shocking in its genius, using <a href="/wiki/Stream_of_consciousness" title="Stream of consciousness">stream of consciousness</a> in a way I'd never heard before."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Stereogum" title="Stereogum">Stereogum</a></i>, reviewing <i>Operation: Doomsday</i> on its 20th anniversary, noted Dumile's "formative" influence on younger rappers.<sup id="cite_ref-Lyons2019_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons2019-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/El-P" title="El-P">El-P</a> of <a href="/wiki/Run_the_Jewels" title="Run the Jewels">Run the Jewels</a> described him as a "writer's writer",<sup id="cite_ref-pfork_remembered_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pfork_remembered-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Q-Tip_(musician)" title="Q-Tip (musician)">Q-Tip</a> called him "your favorite rapper's favorite rapper".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<p>Dumile's worldview was informed by <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Afrocentrism" title="Afrocentrism">Afrocentrism</a> espoused by <a href="/wiki/African-American_Muslims" title="African-American Muslims">African-American Muslims</a>. His parents raised him and his brother as <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Five-Percent_Nation" title="Five-Percent Nation">Five-Percent Nation</a>, a religious black nationalist movement influenced by Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsu200548_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsu200548-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Dumile's father taught him about <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">pan-African</a> history, including historical figures such as <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Garvey" title="Marcus Garvey">Marcus Garvey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Muhammad" title="Elijah Muhammad">Elijah Muhammad</a>—lessons that he then strove to impart upon his peers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPappademas200497_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPappademas200497-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> By the early 1990s, Dumile and the other members of KMD identified as a member of the Ansaar Allah Community, later known as the <a href="/wiki/Nuwaubian_Nation" title="Nuwaubian Nation">Nuwaubian Nation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In their music, the members of KMD professed a religious message based on tenets of Nuwaubianism, which Dumile distinguished from Five-Percent beliefs in an early interview.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilder199137_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilder199137-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In the music video for "Peachfuzz", Dumile and the other members of KMD wear <a href="/wiki/Kufi" title="Kufi">kufi</a> caps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPappademas200496_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPappademas200496-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> By 2000, though he was no longer as strictly observant, Dumile still participated in Nuwaubian events such as the Savior's Day celebration at the <a href="/wiki/Tama-Re" title="Tama-Re">Tama-Re</a> compound in Georgia and held a positive opinion of the community.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dumile was married to Jasmine Dumile for an unknown period until his death in October 2020. The couple had five children.<sup id="cite_ref-leedslive_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leedslive-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Dumile's son Malachi died at the age of 14 from unspecified causes in late 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Dumile lived in the United States for most of his life, he never became a <a href="/wiki/Naturalization" title="Naturalization">naturalized</a> citizen.<sup id="cite_ref-faceddeportation_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faceddeportation-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> He acquired a <a href="/wiki/British_passport" title="British passport">British passport</a> prior to his 2010 European tour; after completing the tour later that year, he was refused re-entry to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Yates2012_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates2012-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The European tour was only his second international tour, and he had previously avoided leaving the United States; he had believed he would be able to secure reentry based on his <a href="/wiki/Permanent_residency" title="Permanent residency">long-term residency</a> and family connections.<sup id="cite_ref-Yates2012_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates2012-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The denial of reentry forced Dumile apart from his wife and children, and for nearly two years he saw them only via video calls or during their brief visits to the United Kingdom. They were reunited when his family moved to London in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-lester2012_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lester2012-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> That year, Dumile said he was "done with the United States".<sup id="cite_ref-Yates2012_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yates2012-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> At the time of his death, he was living in <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-leedslive_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leedslive-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<p>In October 2020, Dumile was admitted to <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_University_Hospital" title="St James&#39;s University Hospital">St James's University Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, England, after suffering respiratory problems.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> On October 31,  he died from <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">angioedema</a>, a rare reaction to a blood pressure medication he had been recently prescribed. He had suffered from <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kidney_disease" title="Kidney disease">kidney disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_lockdown_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="COVID-19 lockdown in the United Kingdom">COVID-19 lockdown</a>, Dumile's wife, Jasmine, was not allowed to visit him in the hospital until the day of his death.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Jasmine announced Dumile's death on December 31, 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The cause of death was not announced until July 2023.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_117-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:8-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many musicians made tributes to Dumile.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> United States President <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a> included Dumile's 2004 track "Coffin Nails" in his <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Joe_Biden" title="Inauguration of Joe Biden">inauguration</a> playlist.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The decision was criticized by some of Dumile's fans, as Biden was part of the <a href="/wiki/Obama_administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Obama administration">Obama administration</a>, which had barred him from returning to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Hood" title="Mr. Hood">Mr. Hood</a></i> (1991, as Zev Love X with <a href="/wiki/KMD" title="KMD">KMD</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Black_Bastards" title="Black Bastards">Black Bastards</a></i> (2000, as Zev Love X with KMD)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Madvillainy" title="Madvillainy">Madvillainy</a></i> (2004, with <a href="/wiki/Madlib" title="Madlib">Madlib</a> as <a href="/wiki/Madvillain" title="Madvillain">Madvillain</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Special_Herbs_%2B_Spices_Volume_1" title="Special Herbs + Spices Volume 1">Special Herbs + Spices Volume 1</a></i> (2004, with <a href="/wiki/MF_Grimm" title="MF Grimm">MF Grimm</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Mouse_and_the_Mask" title="The Mouse and the Mask">The Mouse and the Mask</a></i> (2005, with <a href="/wiki/Danger_Mouse_(musician)" title="Danger Mouse (musician)">Danger Mouse</a> as <a href="/wiki/Danger_Doom" title="Danger Doom">Danger Doom</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Key_to_the_Kuffs" title="Key to the Kuffs">Key to the Kuffs</a></i> (2012, with <a href="/wiki/Jneiro_Jarel" title="Jneiro Jarel">Jneiro Jarel</a> as JJ Doom)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/NehruvianDoom" title="NehruvianDoom">NehruvianDoom</a></i> (2014, with <a href="/wiki/Bishop_Nehru" title="Bishop Nehru">Bishop Nehru</a> as NehruvianDoom)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Czarface_Meets_Metal_Face" title="Czarface Meets Metal Face">Czarface Meets Metal Face</a></i> (2018, with <a href="/wiki/Czarface" title="Czarface">Czarface</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Super_What%3F" title="Super What?">Super What?</a></i> (2021, with Czarface)</li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dumile's <a href="/wiki/Birth_certificate" title="Birth certificate">birth certificate</a> provides his name as "Dumile Daniel Thompson". Variations like "Daniel Dumile Thompson" and "Dumile Thompson Dumile" appear in other records. In his life as a public figure, "Daniel Dumile" was the name most commonly used to refer to him.<sup id="cite_ref-yoo2021_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoo2021-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources differ on when precisely Dumile first performed with his face obscured.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Also spelled "Ghidora"<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> or "Ghidra".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
